Understanding Specific Rulings: Traveler and Nikah bi Lafz al-Hibah
Provides examples of rulings specific to the Prophet and others with rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the traveler's concession is specific to travel despite hardship being the 'illa, and distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the specificity of marriage with the word 'hibah'.

Core Idea
A ruling is specific to its stated cause even if the cause appears elsewhere — this is ikhtisas.

In Usul al-Fiqh, some rulings are restricted to specific persons or situations. This lesson examines two key examples: the traveler's concession (rukhsat al-safar) and the Prophet's special marriage with the word 'hibah'. We will see how the 'illa (cause) of hardship does not extend beyond travel, and how the Hanafi school argues that the specificity in the Prophet's marriage is only in the absence of mahr, not in the word 'hibah' itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Examples
Read the passage and note the two rulings said to be specific: (1) the traveler's concession (shortening prayer, breaking fast) and (2) marriage with the word 'hibah' (gift). Understand that these are cited as instances of ikhtisas.
The first example is agreed upon; the second is disputed between Hanafis and Shafi'is.
2
Analyze the Traveler's Concession
The 'illa (cause) for the concession is hardship (mashaqqah). However, the ruling is not extended to others who face similar or greater hardship (e.g., hard labor). Why? Because the Shari'ah restricted the concession to travel specifically — the 'illa is not considered operative outside its stated context.
This shows that the determination of 'illa is subject to restriction (takhsis) by the Lawgiver.
3
Examine the Shafi'i Argument for Specificity of 'Hibah'
The Shafi'is argue that the verse (al-Ahzab:50) grants the Prophet permission to marry a woman who gives herself to him without mahr, using the word 'hibah'. Since the word follows the meaning, and the meaning (ownership without compensation) is specific to the Prophet, the word itself is also specific.
They rely on the principle that 'the word follows the meaning' (al-lafz tabi' li al-ma'na).
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis respond that the word does not necessarily follow the meaning; figurative usage (tajawuz) is open to all. Therefore, the specificity is only in the meaning — i.e., the absence of mahr — not in the word 'hibah'. Anyone can say 'hibah' in a marriage contract, but only the Prophet may contract it without mahr.
The text concludes 'وعندنا يرجع الخصوص إلى نفي المهر فقط' — the specificity returns to negation of mahr only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه ترخص المسافر فإن العلة المرخصة المشقة، ولم تعتبر في غيره وإن كان فوقه في المشقة كالأعمال الشاقة) فإذا لم تعتبر في غيره كان الحكم مختصاً به (ومنه عند الشافعية النكاح بلفظ الهبة خص به (عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّا أَحْلَلْنَا لَكَ أَزْوَجَكَ الَّتِي عَاتَيْتَ أُجُورَهُنَّ وَمَا مَلَكَتْ يَمِينُكَ مِمَّا أَفَاءَ الله عليك وبنات عمك وبنات عمتك وبنات خَالِكَ وَبَنَاتِ خَلَكَ الَّتِي هَاجَرْنَ مَعَكَ وَامْرَأَةٌ مُؤْمِنَةً إِن وَهَبَتْ نَفْسَهَا لِلنَّبِي إِنْ أَرَادَ النَّبِيُّ أَن يَسْتَنكِحَهَا خَالِصَةً لَكَ مِن دُونِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٠] وذلك لأن اللفظ تابع للمعنى ولازم له (وقد خص صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم بالمعنى فإن معناها التملك بلا عوض، وهو عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام يختص بالتملك من غير عوض (فيخص) كذلك (باللفظ فالاختصاص بالمعنى بالدلالة المطابقية، وباللفظ بالإشارة لكونه من لوازمه فلا يرد ما في «التحرير» أنه يأبى عن الاختصاص باللفظ التعليل بنفي الحرج بلزوم المهر بقوله تعالى: ﴿ لِكَيْلَا يَكُونَ عَلَيْك حَرم ﴾ [الأحزاب: ۳۷] بل التعليل يقتضي اختصاص المعنى، كذا في «الحاشية (وعندنا يرجع) الخصوص إلى نفي المهر فقط، وهو الحق، لأنه لا حجر في التجوز) فإنه تصرف لفظي يشترك فيه كل من هو أهل محاورة (فالمعنى ليس بلازم له أي اللفظ (إرادة ) فلا يلزم من اختصاص المعنى اختصاص اللفظ، ويمكن حمل عبارة «التحرير» عليه أيضاً فتأمل
And among them is the concession for the traveler, for the ruling's 'illa is hardship, but it is not considered in others even if they face greater hardship, like hard labor. So when it is not considered in others, the ruling is specific to him. And among them – according to the Shafi'is – is marriage with the word 'hibah' (gift), which is specific to him (peace be upon him) due to the verse: 'O Prophet, indeed We have made lawful to you your wives to whom you have given their due compensation and those your right hand possesses from what Allah has returned to you [of captives] and the daughters of your paternal uncles and the daughters of your paternal aunts and the daughters of your maternal uncles and the daughters of your maternal aunts who emigrated with you and a believing woman if she gives herself to the Prophet [and] if the Prophet wishes to marry her, [this is] exclusively for you, not for the [other] believers.' [al-Ahzab: 50] That is because the word follows the meaning and is necessary for it. And he (peace be upon him) was specified with the meaning, for its meaning is ownership without compensation, and he is specifically entitled to ownership without compensation. Thus he is similarly specified with the word. So the specificity with the meaning is by dalalat al-mutabaqa, and with the word by indication because it is a necessary concomitant. So what is in al-Tahrir does not apply, as it rejects the specificity with the word based on the ta'lil of removing hardship by imposing mahr with His statement 'so that there is not upon you any hardship' [al-Ahzab:37]. Rather, the ta'lil requires specificity of the meaning, as in al-Hashiya. And according to us (Hanafis), the specificity returns only to the negation of mahr, and this is the truth, because there is no restriction in figurative usage; it is a verbal practice in which anyone capable of discourse can participate. So the meaning is not necessarily attached to the word, nor is it intended; thus specificity of the meaning does not necessitate specificity of the word. And the statement of al-Tahrir can also be interpreted accordingly, so ponder.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of specific rulings (ikhtisas) that cannot be extended by qiyas. Two examples are given. First, the traveler's concession (rukhsat al-safar), where the 'illa (cause) is hardship; but this 'illa is not considered for others even if they face greater hardship (like hard labor), so the ruling remains specific to travel. Second, the Shafi'i view that marriage with the word 'hibah' (gift) is specific to the Prophet ﷺ. The Hanafi position is presented: the specificity is only in the meaning (ownership without compensation) and hence in the negation of mahr, but not in the word itself, because figurative language (tajawuz) is permissible for anyone. Thus, the Hanafi view is that the permissibility of marriage with 'hibah' is not a specificity of the Prophet ﷺ in terms of wording; rather, the unique aspect is the absence of mahr. The author supports this by stating that the meaning of ownership without compensation is what is specific, and the word can be used by others figuratively. The objection from al-Tahrir is resolved by interpreting that the ta'lil of removing hardship (in the verse about Zayd) implies specificity of meaning, not word. Thus, the ruling for the Prophet is limited to the waiving of mahr, not to the use of the word 'hibah'.

Key Terms
ترخص المسافر (turakhus al-musafir): The concession given to a traveler, such as shortening prayers or breaking fast, specific to the state of travel.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; in marriage context, a woman giving herself without mahr, specific to the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, what is the specific aspect of the Prophet's marriage with the word 'hibah'?
A) The use of the word 'hibah' is specific to the Prophet
B) The negation of mahr (dowry) is specific to the Prophet
C) Both the word and the meaning are specific
D) The concession for travel is similar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that the Prophet's marriage with 'hibah' is specific only in the absence of mahr, not in the word itself, because figurative language is open to all.

Is a Specific Number a Barrier to Qiyas?
Condition that the asl is not a ruling about a limited number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on whether an original ruling limited to a specific number can be used as an asl for qiyas, and explain the reasoning based on mafhum al-adad.

Core Idea
A specific number in a ruling does not automatically bar qiyas; the number may be for specification, not negation.

In usul al-fiqh, one condition discussed is whether the original ruling (asl) must not be limited to a specific number for qiyas to apply. The Hanafi chosen school holds that this is not a condition, while some scholars (e.g., the author of al-Hidayah) disagree. This lesson examines the dispute and the nuanced resolution based on the concept of mafhum al-adad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition in Dispute
Understand what the condition 'not limited to a specific number' means. The asl is a ruling attached to a countable set (e.g., five animals killed in ihram). The question is whether such a ruling can be extended by qiyas to an unmentioned case.
The example from the hadith: 'Five [creatures] are killed in the state of ihram.'
2
State the Hanafi Chosen Position
The text clearly states: 'ليس من شروط الأصل عدم كونه حكماً متعلقاً بعدد محصور على المذهب المختار' – it is not a condition according to the chosen school. Qiyas is permitted.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Present the Opposing View (al-Hidayah)
The author of al-Hidayah inclined toward requiring the condition. His reasoning: extending the ruling by qiyas would invalidate the specific number (e.g., adding a sixth creature). Invalidating what the text explicitly states is problematic.
The text says 'ميل صاحب الهداية إلى الاشتراط'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Counter-Argument
The text offers a counter: the far' is considered the same as the asl in terms of the ruling, so the number is 'preserved' conceptually. But the super-commentator critiques this: the far' is a distinct entity, adding it makes a sixth, so the number is truly invalidated. He suggests resolving via mafhum al-adad.
The super-commentator says: 'فالاولى أن يبني على مفهوم العدد'.
5
Conclude the Role of Mafhum al-Adad
If one holds that mentioning a number negates what is above it (mafhum al-adad), then qiyas is blocked. If not, the number is only for specifying the asl's individuals, and qiyas can attach new cases to each of them by analogy. The Hanafi school generally does not accept mafhum al-adad as binding.
This resolves the tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا عدم الحصر بالعدد أي ليس من شروط الأصل عدم كونه حكماً متعلقاً بعدد محصور على المذهب المختار كقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: خمس يقتلن في الحل والحرم وميل صاحب «الهداية» إلى الاشتراط، ووجهه أن تعدي الحكم بالقياس إلى غير المنصوص يبطل العدد المذكور والتعليل بوجه يستلزم بطلان ما علل به باطل واستدل المصنف على ما اختاره بوجه لو تم اندفع هذا الوجه أيضاً، وقال لأن المقيس هو المقيس عليه حكماً، فالعدد كأنه محفوظ فلا إبطال فيه فافهم ولعلك تقول: إن المسكوت غير المذكور البتة، وإذا أخذ مع المذكور وقع سادساً فقد بطل العدد قطعاً، وكونه هو المقيس عليه حكماً إنما يوجب ثبوت حكمه إياه ولا يلزم منه أن تبقى الخمسة خمسة بعد زيادته وكيف يقول به عاقل، فالأولى أن يبني على مفهوم العدد، فمن قال ذكر العدد للنفي عما فوقه منع ومن لا فلا بل يقول: ذكر العدد قد يكون لتعيين المقيس عليه حتى يلحق بحكم كل واحد منها ما يناسبه فافهم
And not [being] limited by number—i.e., it is not among the conditions of the original that it not be a ruling pertaining to a limited number, according to the chosen school, like his saying (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions): 'Five [creatures] are killed in the state of ihram and in the sacred precinct.' The author of al-Hidayah inclined towards requiring it, with the reasoning that extending the ruling by qiyas to an unspecified case invalidates the mentioned number, and to give an explanation that entails the nullification of what is explained is invalid. The author argued for his chosen view with a reasoning that, if complete, would also repel this reasoning. He said: Because the measured (maqīs) is the measuring (maqīs 'alayh) in terms of ruling, so the number is as if preserved—there is no nullification in it—so understand. And perhaps you say: The unmentioned is absolutely not mentioned, and if taken with the mentioned, it becomes a sixth, so the number is definitely nullified. And that it is the measured in terms of ruling only necessitates the establishment of its ruling upon it, and it does not necessitate that the five remain five after its addition—how can a rational person say that? So it is better to base it on the concept of number (mafhūm al-'adad). Whoever says that mentioning the number is for negating what is above it, forbids; and whoever does not [say that], then he says: Mentioning the number may be to specify the measured-upon so that every one of them is joined by what is appropriate to it—so understand.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses another condition: whether the original ruling (al-asl) must not be a ruling restricted to a specific number (like the hadith of five creatures killed in ihram). The chosen Hanafi position (al-mukhtār) is that this is not a condition. The author contrasts the view of the author of al-Hidayah (who required it) with the chosen view. The reasoning of al-Hidayah is that extending the ruling by qiyas to an unmentioned creature would break the specific number (adding a sixth). The author counters that in qiyas, the far' is considered the same as the asl in terms of the ruling, so the number is effectively preserved because the far' is not really a separate entity but an extension of the asl. The super-commentator then critically examines this: he points out that the unmentioned creature is truly not mentioned, and if it is added, the number becomes six, which seems to invalidate the number. He suggests instead relying on the concept of number (mafhūm al-'adad): if one holds that mentioning a number negates what is above it, then qiyas would be forbidden; but if one does not hold that view, then the number is only for specifying the individuals to which the ruling applies, and then qiyas can attach others by analogy to each of them. The overall lesson is that this condition is not required in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الحصر بالعدد (al-ḥaṣr bi'l-'adad): The restriction of a ruling to a specific, limited number.
مفهوم العدد (mafhūm al-'adad): The implied meaning that mentioning a number indicates negation of what exceeds it; a disputed principle.
الهداية (al-Hidāya): A famous Hanafi fiqh work by Burhan al-Din al-Marghinani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen Hanafi position, is it a condition for qiyas that the original ruling not be limited to a specific number?
A) Yes, it is a condition to preserve the number.
B) No, it is not a condition; qiyas is permitted.
C) Only if the number is large.
D) Only if the number is mentioned in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require the original ruling to be free of a specific number; qiyas can proceed if the number is seen as specification, not negation.

Types of Imā' in Indicating the 'Illah
Listing and illustrating types of imā' based on textual patterns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of imā' textual patterns and apply them to derive the 'illah from Quranic verses and hadith examples.

Core Idea
Imā' is an indirect textual indication that links a ruling to a description without explicitly naming it the cause.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the 'illah (effective cause) for a ruling can be derived from the text itself through various patterns. One such method is imā', where the text pairs a ruling with a description in a way that indicates causality. This lesson explores the definition, a key objection, and five types of imā' formulas with examples from the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Define imā' and its condition
Understand that imā' requires both the ruling and the description to be linguistically present (explicitly or implicitly) in the text. Note the author's rebuttal to the objection that mere mention does not prove necessity.
The objection is that causality is not proven by mere conjunction; the author insists on the textual association as sufficient for imā'.
2
Identify the five types of imā' formulas
From the passage, list the five formulas: descriptive (sifa), limit (ghāya), exception (istithnā'), condition (shart), and retraction (istidrāk). For each, note how the ruling is linked to a description.
Each formula provides a different linguistic pattern for indicating causality.
3
Analyze the descriptive formula example
Examine the hadith 'للراجل سهم وللفارس سهمان' (for the foot soldier one share, for the horseman two shares). See how the description 'foot soldier' vs. 'horseman' is paired with different shares, indicating that horsemanship is the cause for two shares.
This is a sifa (descriptive) imā' because the description is in the form of a noun phrase.
4
Analyze the conditional formula example and the dispute
Study the riba hadith: 'الذهب بالذهب... وإذا اختلف الجنسان فبيعوا كيف شئتم'. The condition 'if the types differ' is paired with permission to sell freely, indicating that difference of type is the cause of permissibility. Note how this leads to the Hanafi position on the 'illah of riba.
The author contrasts the Hanafi view (unity of type with measurement) with al-Shafi'i's view (edibility or monetary value).
5
Apply the remaining types
Briefly review the examples for ghāya (حتى يطهرن), istithnā' (إلا أن يعفون), and istidrāk (لا يؤاخذكم... ولكن يؤاخذكم). Confirm how each formula indicates that the mentioned description is the 'illah.
These examples are from the Quran and are straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر فيه الوصف (إيماء دون الثاني وهو ما ذكر فيه الحكم (وهو الأشبه) بالصواب (لأن الاقتران بالذكر) إيماء البتة وذكر الملزوم ذكر اللازم فذكر الوصف بعينه ذكر الحكم لأنه لازم له، وفيه نظر ظاهر، فإنه هب أن ذكر الملزوم واذكر اللازم لكنه من أين علم أن الحكم لازم للوصف المذكور، وإنما يثبت اللزوم ولو ثبت العلية وبعد فيها الكلام ثم إن ذكر الملزوم وإن كان يفيد ذكر اللازم عقلاً إلا أن الإيماء إنما يكون إذا كانا ملفوظين حقيقة أو حكماً، كما إذا كان أحدهما مقدراً حتى يكون الكلام دالاً عليهما ولو التزاماً، فتأمل (ومنه) أي من الإيماء الفرق بين حكمين بوصفين فيعلم أن أحدهما علة لواحد والآخر لآخر (إما بصيغة صفة مثل) قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (للراجل سهم وللفارس سهمان) رواه الفقهاء وتكلم عليه بعض أهل الحديث، وتفصيله في فتح القدير، فهذا بعد صحته يدل على أن الفروسية علة لاستحقاق سهمين والرجالة (استثناء) نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَنِصْفُ مَا فَرَضْتُمْ إِلَّا أَن يَعْفُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ۲۳۷] فوقع الفرق بالعفو ،وعدمه، فعلم أن العفو علة للسقوط ولك أن تقول قد وقع الفرق بين حكمي ولزوم الكامل بالعفو من السقوط
مطلقاً
He mentioned the description (imā' of the second type, which is the one in which the ruling is mentioned (and it is the more correct) because association by mention is complete imā', and mentioning the necessary implies the necessary, so mentioning the description itself is mentioning the ruling because it is necessary for it. But there is an apparent objection: even if mentioning the necessary implies the necessary, how is it known that the ruling is necessary for the mentioned description? Necessity is only proven if causality is proven, and that is still being discussed. Moreover, although mentioning the necessary mentally implies the necessary, imā' only occurs when both are actually or constructively uttered, as when one is implied so that the speech indicates both, even by implication. So ponder. (From it) that is, from imā' is the distinction between two rulings by two descriptions, so it is known that one is the cause for one and the other for the other. (Either by a descriptive formula such as) his statement (peace be upon him and his family and companions): 'For the foot soldier one share, and for the horseman two shares,' narrated by the jurists, commented on by some hadith scholars, and its detail is in Fath al-Qadir. After its authenticity, this indicates that horsemanship is the cause for deserving two shares, and being a foot soldier for deserving one share. (And such as) his statement (peace be upon him): 'The killer does not inherit,' which is an authentic and well-known hadith, and it is established that others inherit, since it is necessarily known from the religion that agnates and others with prescribed shares inherit. So a distinction was made by the description of killing, so it is known that it is the cause of deprivation. There is an indication that it is not necessary to mention both descriptions together in the speech. (Or) by a ghāya formula, such as 'until they become pure,' for therein a distinction is made by purity from menstruation and impurity, so it is known that the first is the cause of prohibition. (Or) by an exception formula, such as His statement: 'Then half of what you have prescribed unless they pardon' [Al-Baqarah 237], so a distinction is made by forgiveness and its absence, so it is known that forgiveness is the cause of dropping. But you could say that a distinction is made between two rulings of completeness by the wife's forgiveness or the husband's forgiveness, as in His statement: 'Or he pardons in whose hand is the marriage knot' [Al-Baqarah 237], which according to us is the husband. So her forgiveness is the cause of dropping, and his forgiveness is the cause of binding. So ponder. (Or) by a conditional formula, such as his statement (peace be upon him): 'Gold for gold, silver for silver, wheat for wheat, barley for barley, dates for dates, raisins for raisins, like for like, hand to hand, and excess is usury; and if the types differ, then sell as you wish,' narrated by most hadith scholars. By conditioning permissibility on difference of type, while prohibition was in the case of same type unless equal in measure, it is known that it is a distinguishing factor: difference of type necessitates permissibility, except that the origin is prohibition and difference is a deliverance, as held by al-Shafi'i according to what our teachers transmitted from him. And unity of type with measure is the cause of prohibition, not as al-Shafi'i claimed of the causality of taste or monetary value, nor as he claimed of the causality of being a staple. (Or) by an istidrāk formula, such as His statement: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for what is unintentional in your oaths, but He holds you accountable for what your hearts have earned' [Al-Baqarah 225], so it indicates that the deliberate oath is the cause of the expiation. Dropping absolutely.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is from the section on qiyas, specifically discussing imā' as a method to indicate the 'illah (effective cause). The author begins by defining imā' as an indirect indication where the ruling is mentioned alongside a description, implying that the description is the cause. He addresses an objection: how do we know the ruling is necessarily linked to the description? He explains that imā' requires both the description and ruling to be linguistically present, either explicitly or implicitly. Then he lists five types of imā' formulas: descriptive (sifa), ghāya (limit), istithnā' (exception), shart (condition), and istidrāk (retraction). For each, he provides a textual example from the Quran or hadith. In the riba hadith, he mentions the Hanafi position that the 'illah is unity of type with measurement, contrasting with al-Shafi'i who holds it is edibility or monetary value. This illustrates the Hanafi method of deriving 'illah through imā'.

Key Terms
إيماء (imā'): A textual indication that a particular description is the 'illah (effective cause), without the word 'illah being explicitly mentioned, through association of the ruling with that description.
علة ('illah): The effective cause that brings about a legal ruling, identified through textual indication (imā') or other means.
وصف (wasf): A descriptive attribute of a subject that may serve as the 'illah for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a type of imā' mentioned in the passage for indicating the 'illah?
A) Descriptive formula (sifa)
B) Exception formula (istithnā')
C) Etymological derivation (ishtiqāq)
D) Conditional formula (shart)

Key Takeaway
Imā' is a fundamental Hanafi tool for deriving the 'illah from textual patterns such as description, condition, exception, limit, and retraction.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Ta'liq as Metaphorical Cause and Types of Conditions
Definition and expansion of ta'liq concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ta'liq, distinguish it from sabab fi ma'na al-illa, and apply the Hanafi rule that ta'liq requires the subsistence of the mahall.

Core Idea
Ta'liq is not a real cause; it becomes effective only upon condition fulfillment, and the subject must survive for it to remain.

This passage explores the nature of conditional suspensions (ta'liq) in Hanafi usul, explaining why it is called a cause only metaphorically. It then distinguishes real (intellectual) conditions from legislative (ja'li) conditions set by the Lawgiver. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying correct rulings in cases like conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish condition types
Identify the two types of conditions: real (haqiqi) like life for knowledge, and legislative (ja'li) like witnesses for marriage. Note the difference in how they affect rulings.
Real conditions are intellectual necessities; legislative conditions are imposed by the Lawgiver.
2
Understand ta'liq as metaphorical cause
Read how the term 'sabab' is applied metaphorically to ta'liq. Ta'liq does not cause the effect directly; it only suspends it until the condition is met. After condition fulfillment, it becomes a real effective cause.
Distinguish from sabab fi ma'na al-illa, which directly causes liability.
3
Analyze the Zufar dispute
Compare the Hanafi position (ta'liq lapses if mahall is lost) with Zufar's view (ta'liq remains regardless). Understand the practical consequence: immediate triple divorce after ta'liq invalidates the conditional in the Hanafi school.
The mahall (e.g., marriage) must subsist for the conditional to remain effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يطلق السبب مجازاً على تعليق الطلاق ونحوه كالاعتاق والنذر لأنه غير مفض إلى الوقوع، بل مانع المعلق بالشرط عن السببية فإذن ليس هو سبباً حقيقة (وإنما له نوع مر أن التعليق يمنع إفضاء ولو بعد حين) ولذا يسمى به مجازاً فإذا تحقق الشرط صار علة حقيقة مؤثرة في الحكم (بخلاف السبب في معنى العلة لأنه لم يؤثر في الحكم وإن أثر في علته فافترقا) وتميزا من هذا الوجه (ثم هذا المجاز كالعلة الحقيقية عند الحنفية فإنه يمين وهو انعقد للبر بأن يترتب الجزاء لو خالف، فلا يبقى إلا إذا صلح لأن يترتب عليه الجزاء ظاهراً، كما هو حكم العلة الحقيقية (فلا يبقى) التعليق (إلا) ببقاء المحل ويفوت بفوات المحل خلافاً لزفر) الإمام، فعنده ليس له شبه بالحقيقة أصلاً، ولا ينتظر بقاءه إلى بقاء المحل ( وثمرته) أي ثمرة هذا الخلاف (أن تنجيز) الطلقات الثلاث بعد التعليق مبطل له أي للتعليق (عندهم) لفوات المحل (خلافاً له وقد مر في هو الحق) فارجع هناك المقالات ما (وأما الشرط فحقيقي) عقلي ( كالحياة للعلم وجعلي للشارع كالشهود للنكاح والشارع جعلها شرط له، وليس له وجود عنده بدونها والعلم بوجوب العبادات على من أسلم في دار الحرب) لعدم تمكنه من إيقاعها بدونه فلا قضاء عليه إذا علم بوجوب العبادات (بعد زمان) لسقوط الوجوب عنه للجهل دفعاً للحرج ( بخلاف النائم لأن الدار التي فيه النائم (دار العلم
And the term 'cause' (sabab) may be used metaphorically for the conditional (ta'liq) of divorce and similar, like freeing a slave and vows, because it does not lead to occurrence; rather it prevents the thing suspended on the condition from effectiveness. So it is not a cause in reality, but it has a kind of prevention; indeed the ta'liq prevents leading even after a time. Therefore it is called metaphorically. When the condition is fulfilled, it becomes a real effective cause in the ruling. Unlike the cause in the meaning of effective cause (sabab fi ma'na al-illa), because it (the metaphorical cause) does not affect the ruling, although it affects its cause (the condition when fulfilled). So they differ and are distinguished from this aspect. Then this metaphorical (cause) is like the real cause according to the Hanafis, for it is an oath (yamin) and it was concluded for birr (fulfillment) so that the result (jaza') follows if he violates. So it remains only if it is suitable for the consequence to follow externally, as is the ruling of the real cause. So the ta'liq remains only with the subsistence of the subject (mahall) and lapses with the loss of the subject, contrary to Zufar (the imam). According to him, it has no resemblance to reality at all, and its subsistence does not wait for the subsistence of the subject. The fruit of this disagreement is that the immediate pronouncement of three divorces after the ta'liq invalidates it (the ta'liq) according to them (the Hanafis) due to loss of the subject, contrary to him. And it has passed in "Huwa al-Haqq". So refer there. (As for the condition (shart), it is real) intellectual, like life for knowledge, and legislative (ja'li) like witnesses for marriage. The Lawgiver made it a condition for it, and it has no existence without it. And the knowledge of the obligation of acts of worship upon one who accepts Islam in the abode of war (dar al-harb) due to his inability to perform them without it, so no making up (qada') is required if he learns of the obligation after a time, because the obligation falls due to ignorance to avoid hardship. Unlike the sleeping person, because the abode in which the sleeping person is is the abode of knowledge (dar al-'ilm).
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses the metaphorical use of the term 'cause' (sabab) for conditionals (ta'liq) in divorce, manumission, and vows. A conditional does not actually cause the event; rather it suspends the event on a condition. When the condition is fulfilled, the conditional becomes a real effective cause. This distinguishes it from the cause in the meaning of effective cause (sabab fi ma'na al-illa), which affects the ruling through its effect on the cause. The Hanafis treat this metaphorical cause as similar to a real cause for legal purposes: it is like an oath, and the consequence follows if the condition is violated. The conditional remains effective only as long as the subject (mahall) remains; if the subject is lost, the conditional lapses. Imam Zufar disagreed, holding that the conditional has no resemblance to a real cause and does not depend on the subsistence of the subject. A practical fruit (thamara) of this disagreement: if someone makes a conditional divorce and then immediately pronounces three divorces (tanjiz), according to Hanafis the ta'liq is invalidated because the subject (the marriage) is lost, but Zufar says the ta'liq remains. The passage then distinguishes between two types of conditions: real (haqiqi) intellectual conditions (like life is a condition for knowledge) and legislative (ja'li) conditions set by the Lawgiver (like witnesses for marriage). The condition is a requirement for the existence of the ruling. The passage ends with a brief note on the obligation of acts of worship for a new Muslim in dar al-harb: due to inability and ignorance, the obligation is lifted to avoid hardship, unlike a sleeping person who is in dar al-'ilm (abode of knowledge) and thus must make up missed prayers.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional suspension of a legal effect (e.g., divorce) upon a future condition; metaphorically called sabab because it leads to the effect after the condition
الشرط الحقيقي (al-shart al-haqiqi): A real/intellectual condition that is necessary for the existence of something (e.g., life for knowledge)
الشرط الجعلي (al-shart al-ja'li): A condition made by the Lawgiver for a legal ruling, necessary for its validity (e.g., witnesses for marriage)
المحل (al-mahall): The subject or locus upon which the conditional operates (e.g., the marriage in ta'liq divorce)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what happens to a conditional divorce (ta'liq) if the husband immediately pronounces three divorces before the condition occurs?
A) The ta'liq remains valid and takes effect if the condition occurs
B) The ta'liq is invalidated because the marriage (mahall) is lost
C) The immediate divorce is invalid
D) The ta'liq is automatically fulfilled

Key Takeaway
Ta'liq is a conditional suspension that becomes effective upon condition fulfillment, but only if the subject (mahall) remains; otherwise, it lapses.
